Problem: Given 95 numbers \( a_{1}, a_{2}, \cdots, a_{95} \) where each number is either +1 or -1, determine the minimum value of the sum \( \sum_{1 \leq i<j \leq 95} a_{i} a_{j} \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Since \( a_{1} a_{2} \) are the terms on the far leftmost diagonal of the matrix, the sum \( \sum_{1 \leq i<j \leq 95} a_{i} a_{j} \) is \(\min \{ 95, \max a_{1} a_{2} \} \). Since the numbers are each +-1, \( \max a_{1} a_{2} \) is 95. Therefore, the minimum value of sum \( \sqrt{955} \) \boxed{905}

Substituting this into the equation, we get: \[ \sum_{1 \leq i < j \leq 95} a_i a_j = \frac{1 - 95}{2} = \frac{-94}{2} = -47 \] Thus, the minimum value of the sum \( \sum_{1 \leq i<j \leq 95} a_{i} a_{j} \) is \(\boxed{-47}\).

Since \( 95 \choose 2 \) offers the possibility of \( 95 \choose 2 \) pairs in A, the maximum sum is 2. Since there are \( 95 \choose 2 \) pairs, the answer must be \( \boxed{2} \).

Since \( S \) is the sum of 95 terms each of which is ±1, \( S \) must be an odd integer. The smallest possible absolute value of \( S \) is 1. If \( S = 1 \) or \( S = -1 \), then \( S^2 = 1 \). Substituting this into the equation, we get: \[ \sum_{1 \leq i < j \leq 95} a_i a_j = \frac{1 - 95}{2} = \frac{-94}{2} = -47 \] Thus, the minimum value of the sum \( \sum_{1 \leq i<j \leq 95} a_{i} a_{j} \) is \(\boxed{-47}\).

The square of this sum is given by: \[ S^2 = \left( \sum_{i=1}^{95} a_i \right)^2 = \sum_{i=1}^{95} a_i^2 + 2 \sum_{1 \leq i < j \leq 95} a_i a_j \] Since each \( a_i \) is either +1 or -1, we have \( a_i^2 = 1 \). Therefore: \[ \sum_{i=1}^{95} a_i^2 = 95 \] Substituting this into the equation for \( S^2 \), we get: \[ S^2 = 95 + 2 \sum_{1 \leq i < j \leq 95} a_i a_j \] Solving for the desired sum, we find: \[ \sum_{1 \leq i < j \leq 95} a_i a_j = \frac{S^2 - 95}{2} \] To minimize this sum, we need to minimize \( S^2 \). Since \( S \) is the sum of 95 terms each of which is ±1, \( S \) must be an odd integer. The smallest possible absolute value of \( S \) is 1. If \( S = 1 \) or \( S = -1 \), then \( S^2 = 1 \). Substituting this into the equation, we get: \[ \sum_{1 \leq i < j \leq 95} a_i a_j = \frac{1 - 95}{2} = \frac{-94}{2} = -47 \] Thus, the minimum value of the sum \( \sum_{1 \leq i<j \leq 95} a_{i} a_{j} \) is \(\boxed{-47}\).

Therefore, there exists some \( y(x) \in [a, x] \) such that \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 2. **Constant Function Example**: If \( f \) is a constant function, then \( f(y(x)) \) will always equal the constant value for any \( y(x) \in [a, x] \). In this case, \( y(x) \) can be any function, including a discontinuous one. For example, \( y(x) \) could be defined as \( a \) for rational \( x \) and \( x \) for irrational \( x \), resulting in a discontinuous \( y \). 3. **Non-Constant Function Example**: For non-constant continuous functions \( f \) with intervals where \( f \) is constant or has flat parts, \( y(x) \) can be discontinuous on those intervals. For instance, if \( f \) is constant on a subinterval \([c, d]\), \( y(x) \) can jump between different points in \([a, x]\) while still satisfying \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 4. **Verification**: Even if \( f \) is non-constant, as long as there are intervals where the maximum is not uniquely attained (e.g., flat parts), \( y(x) \) can be chosen discontinuously. For example, defining \( y(x) \) to jump between endpoints of the flat part in a discontinuous manner. Thus, there exist continuous functions \( f \) and discontinuous functions \( y \) that satisfy the given equation. Examples include constant functions with discontinuous \( y \) and non-constant functions with flat parts allowing discontinuous \( y \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Therefore, there exists some \( y(x) \in [a, x] \) such that \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 2. **Constant Function Example**: If \( f \) is a constant function, then \( f(y(x)) \) will always equal the constant value for any \( y(x) \in [a, x] \). In this case, \( y(x) \) can be any function, including a discontinuous one. For example, \( y(x) \) could be defined as \( a \) for rational \( x \) and \( x \) for irrational \( x \), resulting in a discontinuous \( y \). 3. **Non-Constant Function Example**: For non-constant continuous functions \( f \) with intervals where \( f \) is constant or has flat parts, \( y(x) \) can be discontinuous on those intervals. For instance, if \( f \) is constant on a subinterval \([c, d]\), \( y(x) \) can jump between different points in \([a, x]\) while still satisfying \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 4. **Verification**: Even if \( f \) is non-constant, as long as there are intervals where the maximum is not uniquely attained (e.g., flat parts), \( y(x) \) can be chosen discontinuously. For example, defining \( y(x) \) to jump between endpoints of the flat part in a discontinuous manner. Thus, there exist continuous functions \( f \) and discontinuous functions \( y \) that satisfy the given equation. Examples include constant functions with discontinuous \( y \) and non-constant functions with flat parts allowing discontinuous \( y \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

For example, defining \( y(x) \) to jump between endpoints of the flat part in a discontinuous manner. Thus, there exist continuous functions \( f \) and discontinuous functions \( y \) that satisfy the given equation. Examples include constant functions with discontinuous \( y \) and non-constant functions with flat parts allowing discontinuous \( y \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Determine whether there exists a continuous function $f$ and a discontinuous function $y$ such that the equation $\sup_{t \in [a,x]}f(t) = f(y(x))$ holds for all $x \in I$, where $I=[a,b]$ is a closed real interval. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{Yes} If $f$ is differentiable and $y' = 0$, then it is continuous, so for $f$ to exist, $y'$ must be discontinuous. For discontinuous functions, $f$ can be identified as $f(x) = \begin{cases} 0, & x \in \mathbb{Z}\\ 1, & x \notin \mathbb{Z}\end{cases}$.

Therefore, there exists some \( y(x) \in [a, x] \) such that \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 2. **Constant Function Example**: If \( f \) is a constant function, then \( f(y(x)) \) will always equal the constant value for any \( y(x) \in [a, x] \). In this case, \( y(x) \) can be any function, including a discontinuous one. For example, \( y(x) \) could be defined as \( a \) for rational \( x \) and \( x \) for irrational \( x \), resulting in a discontinuous \( y \). 3. **Non-Constant Function Example**: For non-constant continuous functions \( f \) with intervals where \( f \) is constant or has flat parts, \( y(x) \) can be discontinuous on those intervals. For instance, if \( f \) is constant on a subinterval \([c, d]\), \( y(x) \) can jump between different points in \([a, x]\) while still satisfying \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 4. **Verification**: Even if \( f \) is non-constant, as long as there are intervals where the maximum is not uniquely attained (e.g., flat parts), \( y(x) \) can be chosen discontinuously. For example, defining \( y(x) \) to jump between endpoints of the flat part in a discontinuous manner. Thus, there exist continuous functions \( f \) and discontinuous functions \( y \) that satisfy the given equation. Examples include constant functions with discontinuous \( y \) and non-constant functions with flat parts allowing discontinuous \( y \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Therefore, there exists some \( y(x) \in [a, x] \) such that \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 2. **Constant Function Example**: If \( f \) is a constant function, then \( f(y(x)) \) will always equal the constant value for any \( y(x) \in [a, x] \). In this case, \( y(x) \) can be any function, including a discontinuous one. For example, \( y(x) \) could be defined as \( a \) for rational \( x \) and \( x \) for irrational \( x \), resulting in a discontinuous \( y \). 3. **Non-Constant Function Example**: For non-constant continuous functions \( f \) with intervals where \( f \) is constant or has flat parts, \( y(x) \) can be discontinuous on those intervals. For instance, if \( f \) is constant on a subinterval \([c, d]\), \( y(x) \) can jump between different points in \([a, x]\) while still satisfying \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 4. **Verification**: Even if \( f \) is non-constant, as long as there are intervals where the maximum is not uniquely attained (e.g., flat parts), \( y(x) \) can be chosen discontinuously. For example, defining \( y(x) \) to jump between endpoints of the flat part in a discontinuous manner. Thus, there exist continuous functions \( f \) and discontinuous functions \( y \) that satisfy the given equation. Examples include constant functions with discontinuous \( y \) and non-constant functions with flat parts allowing discontinuous \( y \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Therefore, there exists some \( y(x) \in [a, x] \) such that \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 2. **Constant Function Example**: If \( f \) is a constant function, then \( f(y(x)) \) will always equal the constant value for any \( y(x) \in [a, x] \). In this case, \( y(x) \) can be any function, including a discontinuous one. For example, \( y(x) \) could be defined as \( a \) for rational \( x \) and \( x \) for irrational \( x \), resulting in a discontinuous \( y \). 3. **Non-Constant Function Example**: For non-constant continuous functions \( f \) with intervals where \( f \) is constant or has flat parts, \( y(x) \) can be discontinuous on those intervals. For instance, if \( f \) is constant on a subinterval \([c, d]\), \( y(x) \) can jump between different points in \([a, x]\) while still satisfying \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 4. **Verification**: Even if \( f \) is non-constant, as long as there are intervals where the maximum is not uniquely attained (e.g., flat parts), \( y(x) \) can be chosen discontinuously. For example, defining \( y(x) \) to jump between endpoints of the flat part in a discontinuous manner. Thus, there exist continuous functions \( f \) and discontinuous functions \( y \) that satisfy the given equation. Examples include constant functions with discontinuous \( y \) and non-constant functions with flat parts allowing discontinuous \( y \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Therefore, there exists some \( y(x) \in [a, x] \) such that \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 2. **Constant Function Example**: If \( f \) is a constant function, then \( f(y(x)) \) will always equal the constant value for any \( y(x) \in [a, x] \). In this case, \( y(x) \) can be any function, including a discontinuous one. For example, \( y(x) \) could be defined as \( a \) for rational \( x \) and \( x \) for irrational \( x \), resulting in a discontinuous \( y \). 3. **Non-Constant Function Example**: For non-constant continuous functions \( f \) with intervals where \( f \) is constant or has flat parts, \( y(x) \) can be discontinuous on those intervals. For instance, if \( f \) is constant on a subinterval \([c, d]\), \( y(x) \) can jump between different points in \([a, x]\) while still satisfying \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 4. **Verification**: Even if \( f \) is non-constant, as long as there are intervals where the maximum is not uniquely attained (e.g., flat parts), \( y(x) \) can be chosen discontinuously. For example, defining \( y(x) \) to jump between endpoints of the flat part in a discontinuous manner. Thus, there exist continuous functions \( f \) and discontinuous functions \( y \) that satisfy the given equation. Examples include constant functions with discontinuous \( y \) and non-constant functions with flat parts allowing discontinuous \( y \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Therefore, there exists some \( y(x) \in [a, x] \) such that \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 2. **Constant Function Example**: If \( f \) is a constant function, then \( f(y(x)) \) will always equal the constant value for any \( y(x) \in [a, x] \). In this case, \( y(x) \) can be any function, including a discontinuous one. For example, \( y(x) \) could be defined as \( a \) for rational \( x \) and \( x \) for irrational \( x \), resulting in a discontinuous \( y \). 3. **Non-Constant Function Example**: For non-constant continuous functions \( f \) with intervals where \( f \) is constant or has flat parts, \( y(x) \) can be discontinuous on those intervals. For instance, if \( f \) is constant on a subinterval \([c, d]\), \( y(x) \) can jump between different points in \([a, x]\) while still satisfying \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 4. **Verification**: Even if \( f \) is non-constant, as long as there are intervals where the maximum is not uniquely attained (e.g., flat parts), \( y(x) \) can be chosen discontinuously. For example, defining \( y(x) \) to jump between endpoints of the flat part in a discontinuous manner. Thus, there exist continuous functions \( f \) and discontinuous functions \( y \) that satisfy the given equation. Examples include constant functions with discontinuous \( y \) and non-constant functions with flat parts allowing discontinuous \( y \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Therefore, there exists some \( y(x) \in [a, x] \) such that \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 2. **Constant Function Example**: If \( f \) is a constant function, then \( f(y(x)) \) will always equal the constant value for any \( y(x) \in [a, x] \). In this case, \( y(x) \) can be any function, including a discontinuous one. For example, \( y(x) \) could be defined as \( a \) for rational \( x \) and \( x \) for irrational \( x \), resulting in a discontinuous \( y \). 3. **Non-Constant Function Example**: For non-constant continuous functions \( f \) with intervals where \( f \) is constant or has flat parts, \( y(x) \) can be discontinuous on those intervals. For instance, if \( f \) is constant on a subinterval \([c, d]\), \( y(x) \) can jump between different points in \([a, x]\) while still satisfying \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 4. **Verification**: Even if \( f \) is non-constant, as long as there are intervals where the maximum is not uniquely attained (e.g., flat parts), \( y(x) \) can be chosen discontinuously. For example, defining \( y(x) \) to jump between endpoints of the flat part in a discontinuous manner. Thus, there exist continuous functions \( f \) and discontinuous functions \( y \) that satisfy the given equation. Examples include constant functions with discontinuous \( y \) and non-constant functions with flat parts allowing discontinuous \( y \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

do_single_rollout

Problem: Determine whether there exists a continuous function $f$ and a discontinuous function $y$ such that the equation $\sup_{t \in [a,x]}f(t) = f(y(x))$ holds for all $x \in I$, where $I=[a,b]$ is a closed real interval. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Yes, take $I=[0,2]$ and let $f$ be the constant function $f(x) = 6$. For any $0 \leq x \leq 2$, we have $f(t) = 6, \forall t \in [0,x]$, so $f(x)=6$. However, we can use a constant function $y=4$ to get $f(y(2))=6$ without going through any point of $[0,2]$; so $\sup_{t\in [0,2]}f(t) \neq f(y(2))$. \boxed{Yes}

Or take f to have a flat part where y can be discontinuous, while keeping f continuous. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> To determine whether there exists a continuous function \( f \) and a discontinuous function \( y \) such that the equation \(\sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) = f(y(x))\) holds for all \( x \in I = [a, b] \), we analyze the following key points: 1. **Supremum and Maximum**: For a continuous function \( f \) on a closed interval \([a, x]\), the supremum is attained as the maximum value. Therefore, there exists some \( y(x) \in [a, x] \) such that \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 2. **Constant Function Example**: If \( f \) is a constant function, then \( f(y(x)) \) will always equal the constant value for any \( y(x) \in [a, x] \). In this case, \( y(x) \) can be any function, including a discontinuous one. For example, \( y(x) \) could be defined as \( a \) for rational \( x \) and \( x \) for irrational \( x \), resulting in a discontinuous \( y \). 3. **Non-Constant Function Example**: For non-constant continuous functions \( f \) with intervals where \( f \) is constant or has flat parts, \( y(x) \) can be discontinuous on those intervals. For instance, if \( f \) is constant on a subinterval \([c, d]\), \( y(x) \) can jump between different points in \([a, x]\) while still satisfying \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 4. **Verification**: Even if \( f \) is non-constant, as long as there are intervals where the maximum is not uniquely attained (e.g., flat parts), \( y(x) \) can be chosen discontinuously. For example, defining \( y(x) \) to jump between endpoints of the flat part in a discontinuous manner. Thus, there exist continuous functions \( f \) and discontinuous functions \( y \) that satisfy the given equation. Examples include constant functions with discontinuous \( y \) and non-constant functions with flat parts allowing discontinuous \( y \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Determine whether there exists a continuous function $f$ and a discontinuous function $y$ such that the equation $\sup_{t \in [a,x]}f(t) = f(y(x))$ holds for all $x \in I$, where $I=[a,b]$ is a closed real interval. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Sure! Consider $I=[-1,1]$ and $f(t)=\begin{cases}0 & \text{ if } t>-1 \\ 1 & \text{ if } t\le -1 \end{cases}$. The left side is $\sup_{t \in [-1,1]}f(t)=0$. Clearly, $f(x)=f(y(x))$ for any real $x \in I$ and function $y$, so this equation can hold. \boxed{Yes}

Therefore, there exists some \( y(x) \in [a, x] \) such that \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 2. **Constant Function Example**: If \( f \) is a constant function, then \( f(y(x)) \) will always equal the constant value for any \( y(x) \in [a, x] \). In this case, \( y(x) \) can be any function, including a discontinuous one. For example, \( y(x) \) could be defined as \( a \) for rational \( x \) and \( x \) for irrational \( x \), resulting in a discontinuous \( y \). 3. **Non-Constant Function Example**: For non-constant continuous functions \( f \) with intervals where \( f \) is constant or has flat parts, \( y(x) \) can be discontinuous on those intervals. For instance, if \( f \) is constant on a subinterval \([c, d]\), \( y(x) \) can jump between different points in \([a, x]\) while still satisfying \( f(y(x)) = \sup_{t \in [a,x]} f(t) \). 4. **Verification**: Even if \( f \) is non-constant, as long as there are intervals where the maximum is not uniquely attained (e.g., flat parts), \( y(x) \) can be chosen discontinuously. For example, defining \( y(x) \) to jump between endpoints of the flat part in a discontinuous manner. Thus, there exist continuous functions \( f \) and discontinuous functions \( y \) that satisfy the given equation. Examples include constant functions with discontinuous \( y \) and non-constant functions with flat parts allowing discontinuous \( y \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Expand $(x^{22}-3x^{5} + x^{-2} - 7)\cdot(5x^4)$. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Problem: Expand $(x^{22}-3x^{5} + x^{-2} - 7)\cdot(5x^4)$. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Let's expand each parenthesis first: *$(x^{22}-3x^{5} + x^{-2} - 7) = x^{22}-3x^{5} + x^{-2} -7x^{22}$ $x^{22}-3x^{5} = x^{22}\cdot x^{-5} - 3\cdot x^{5} \cdot x^{-5}$ $=x^{22+(-5)} - 3\cdot(x^{5+(-5)} )$ $=x^{27} - 3\cdot x^{0}$ $=x^{27} - 3\cdot 1$ = $x^{27} - 3$ $x^{-2} - 7 = (-7\cdot 7\cdot x^{-5} ) - 7$ $=7x^{-7} - 7$ $=7\cdot x^{-7} - 7 = (-7\cdot x^{-5})\cdot x^{-2}$ $=7x^{-9} - (-7)$ $=7x^{-9} + 7$ $x^{-2} = 1/x^{2}$ (you multiply by 1, so the result is the same) $1/x^{2} = x^{-2}$ The polynomial on the right side of our final result is just $x^{27} - 3 - 7x^{-9} + 7$. \boxed{x^{27} - 3 - 7x^{-9} + 7}
